<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The bracteatus pineapple genome and domestication of clonally propagated crops"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-09-30"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Domestication of clonally propagated crops such as pineapple from South America was hypothesized to be a &#8216;one-step operation&#8217;. We sequenced the genome of Ananas comosus var. bracteatus CB5 and assembled 513&#8201;Mb into 25 chromosomes with 29,412 genes. Comparison of the genomes of CB5, F153 and MD2 elucidated the genomic basis of fiber production, color formation, sugar accumulation and fruit maturation. We also resequenced 89 Ananas genomes. Cultivars &#8216;Smooth Cayenne&#8217; and &#8216;Queen&#8217; exhibited ancient and recent admixture, while &#8216;Singapore Spanish&#8217; supported a one-step operation of domestication. We identified 25 selective sweeps, including a strong sweep containing a pair of tandemly duplicated bromelain inhibitors. Four candidate genes for self-incompatibility were linked in F153, but were not functional in self-compatible CB5. Our findings support the coexistence of sexual recombination and a one-step operation in the domestication of clonally propagated crops. This work guides the exploration of sexual and asexual domestication trajectories in other clonally propagated crops. Analysis of the bracteatus pineapple genome provides insight into fiber production, color formation, sugar accumulation and fruit maturation. Resequencing of 89 Ananas genomes supports the coexistence of sexual recombination and a &#8216;one-step operation&#8217; during domestication."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Domestication of clonally propagated crops such as pineapple from South America was hypothesized to be a ‘one-step operation’. We sequenced the genome of <i>Ananas comosus</i> var. <i>bracteatus</i> CB5 and assembled 513 Mb into 25 chromosomes with 29,412 genes. Comparison of the genomes of CB5, F153 and MD2 elucidated the genomic basis of fiber production, color formation, sugar accumulation and fruit maturation. We also resequenced 89 <i>Ananas</i> genomes. Cultivars ‘Smooth Cayenne’ and ‘Queen’ exhibited ancient and recent admixture, while ‘Singapore Spanish’ supported a one-step operation of domestication. We identified 25 selective sweeps, including a strong sweep containing a pair of tandemly duplicated bromelain inhibitors. Four candidate genes for self-incompatibility were linked in F153, but were not functional in self-compatible CB5. Our findings support the coexistence of sexual recombination and a one-step operation in the domestication of clonally propagated crops. This work guides the exploration of sexual and asexual domestication trajectories in other clonally propagated crops.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Most grain crops, vegetables and ornamentals are produced sexually through seed propagation, whereas most fruit trees, tubers and some ornamentals are clonally propagated through grafting, tissue culture, divisions or cuttings. Sexually reproducing species undergo hundreds to thousands of generations of recombination during domestication; this recurrent selection leaves highly tractable signatures in the genome. In contrast, domestication of clonally propagated crops depends on both vegetative and sexual reproduction, the latter acting more sporadically on long-lived clones. It can even be a one-step operation, where selection is completed once a clone is selected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zohary, D. Unconscious selection and the evolution of domesticated plants. Econ. Bot. 58, 5–10 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1253">1</a></sup>. Hence, clonal crops may have undergone zero to a few recombination and selection cycles postdomestication, in sharp contrast to sexually reproducing annual crops.</p><p>Pineapple (<i>Ananas comosus</i> (L.) Merr.) is a fruit crop originated and domesticated in South America. According to Bertoni<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bertoni, M. S. Contributions à l'étude Botanique des Plantes Cultivées (Ex Sylvis. Puerto Bertoni, Alto Parana. PY, 1919)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1263">2</a></sup>, the genus name <i>Ananas</i> means ‘excellent fruit’ in the Guaraní language of Paraguay. Pineapple was domesticated &gt;6,000 years ago with archaeobotanical remains dated 3,500 years ago in South America and distributed to Mesoamerica &gt;2,500 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Byers, D. S. Prehistory of the Tehuacan Valley (Univ. of Texas Press, 1967)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1270">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G. &amp; Duval, M.-F. The domestication of pineapple: context and hypotheses. Pineapple News 16, 15–27 (2009)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1270_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G., Uriza Avila, D. E., Rebolledo Martínez, A. &amp; Rebolledo Martínez, L. The Cascajal Block: another testimony of the antiquity of pineapple in Mexico? Pineapple News, 18, 47–48 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1273">5</a></sup>. Pineapple is clonally propagated using the leafy fruit crown, slips or suckers.</p><p>Red pineapple (<i>Ananas comosus</i> var. <i>bracteatus</i>) was anciently cultivated for fiber, fruit juice and as a living hedge, and is now a pantropical ornamental<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Baker, K. F. &amp; Collins, J. L. Notes on the distribution and ecology of Ananas and Pseudananas. Am. J. Bot. 26, 697–702 (1939)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1286">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Duval, M. F., Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G., Ferreira, F. R., Bianchetti, L. D. B. &amp; Cabral, J. R. S. First results from joint EMBRAPA-CIRAD Ananas germplasm collecting in Brazil and French Guyana. Acta Hortic. 425, 137–144 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1289">7</a></sup>. The <i>bracteatus</i> plant is conspicuous for its bright pink-to-red colored fruit. The name ‘<i>bracteatus</i>’ refers to its long bracts. The plant is vigorous with long leaves, coarse spines and abundant suckers. Plant fibers have been used in numerous applications that are beneficial to agriculture and the environment, partly because of their biodegradable nature and lack of carcinogenicity. Pineapple leaf fiber (PALF) contains 70–82% cellulose, 5–12% lignin and 1.1% ash<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Asim, M. et al. A review on pineapple leaves fibre and its composites. Int. J. Polym. Sci. 2015, 950567 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1300">8</a></sup>. PALF is a major source of natural fiber and has been used in the production of activated carbon fibers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Beltrame, K. K. et al. Adsorption of caffeine on mesoporous activated carbon fibers prepared from pineapple plant leaves. Ecotox. Environ. Safe. 147, 64–71 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1304">9</a></sup>, packaging materials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Abd Razak, S. I., Sharif, N. F. A., Nayan, N. H. M., Muhamad, I. I. &amp; Yahya, M. Y. Impregnation of poly(lactic acid) on biologically pulped pineapple leaf fiber for packaging materials. Bioresources 10, 4350–4359 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1308">10</a></sup>, cell scaffolds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Costa, L. M. M. et al. Bionanocomposites from electrospun PVA/pineapple nanofibers/Stryphnodendron adstringens bark extract for medical applications. Ind. Crop. Prod. 41, 198–202 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1312">11</a></sup> and apparels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hazarika, P. et al. Development of apparels from silk waste and pineapple leaf fiber. J. Nat. Fibers 15, 416–424 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1316">12</a></sup>.</p><p>Pineapple is in Bromeliaceae, which includes &gt;3,000 species grouped within &gt;50 genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Benzing, D. H. Bromeliaceae: Profile of an Adaptive Radiation. (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2000)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1323">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Adaptive radiation, correlated and contingent evolution, and net species diversification in Bromeliaceae. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 71, 55–78 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1326">14</a></sup>. Bromeliads challenge classical species concepts because of their notoriously leaky pre- and postzygotic barriers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barbará, T., Martinelli, G., Fay, M., Mayo, S. &amp; Lexer, C. Population differentiation and species cohesion in two closely related plants adapted to neotropical high‐altitude ‘inselbergs’, Alcantarea imperialis and Alcantarea geniculata (Bromeliaceae). Mol. Ecol. 16, 1981–1992 (2007)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1330">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendt, T., Canela, M. B. F., de Faria, A. P. G. &amp; Rios, R. I. Reproductive biology and natural hybridization between two endemic species of Pitcairnia (Bromeliaceae). Am. J. Bot. 88, 1760–1767 (2001)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1330_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Palma-Silva, C. et al. Sympatric bromeliad species (Pitcairnia spp.) facilitate tests of mechanisms involved in species cohesion and reproductive isolation in Neotropical inselbergs. Mol. Ecol. 20, 3185–3201 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1333">17</a></sup>. <i>Ananas</i> is unique in the family for its syncarpic fruit. Variety <i>bracteatus</i> is a cultigen, anciently cultivated for fiber in Southeastern Brazil, Paraguay and northern Argentina. Here, we generated a second <i>Ananas</i> reference genome from the <i>bracteatus</i> accession CB5 and resequenced numerous leading pineapple cultivars and wild <i>Ananas</i> species to explore the diversity and domestication history of pineapple, patterns of clonal propagation and signatures of human selection.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation of <i>bracteatus</i> pineapple accession CB5</h3><p>The genome size of CB5 was estimated to be ~591 Mb by flow cytometry. We generated 26.9 Gb of reads from the PacBio RSII platform and ~100× Illumina short reads. Initial assembly using CANU yielded 809.6 Mb of assembled sequence. To eliminate redundant homozygous sequences, we developed a new algorithm, Pseudohaploid, that identifies and filters out heterozygous contigs based on whole-genome alignment. The resulting assembly was 513 Mb, with a contig N50 of 427 kb at 92.6% completeness and much reduced duplicated sequences (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">2</a>). Alignment of RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) assembled transcripts to the genome revealed 99.92% sequence identity (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">3</a>). Additionally, 98.47% of Illumina reads were aligned to the genome, covering 99.51% of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>Contigs were corrected and scaffolded by high-throughput chromatin conformation capture (Hi-C) into 25 pseudo-chromosomes that anchored 456 Mb (88.8%) of the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">1</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">5</a>). Overall, 29,412 putative protein-coding gene models were annotated (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">6</a>). We identified 383.2 Mb of repetitive sequences, accounting for 74.7% of the assembled genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">7</a>). Kimura distances indicated a burst of long terminal repeat retrotransposon (LTR-RT) activity ~1.8 million years ago.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Distribution of genomic features along the pineapple CB5 genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Distribution of genomic features along the pineapple CB5 genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="691" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>–<b>e</b>, The rings indicate (from outermost to innermost) 25 chromosomes (<b>a</b>), gene density (<b>b</b>), transposable element abundance (<b>c</b>), gene copy number variation (<b>d</b>) and large-scale insertions compared to the F153 genome (<b>e</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Improved assembly of pineapple F153 genome</h3><p>The highest Gypsy LTR-RT content is concentrated near the centromeres in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Wang, H. The contributions of transposable elements to the structure, function, and evolution of plant genomes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 505–530 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1452">18</a></sup>. The distribution of the Gypsy elements was plotted along the 25 pseudomolecules in the F153 genome (referred to as F153 v.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1456">19</a></sup>. Two peaks were observed in the linkage group (LG)01 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">3</a>), a chimeric pseudomolecule corresponding to two chromosomes in CB5 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">4</a>). There was a Gypsy peak at one end of LG24, while there was no Gypsy rich-region in LG25, which align to one chromosome in CB5. The misassembled LGs were corrected in the improved F153 genome assembly (referred to as F153 v.7), in which LG01 was separated into AccChr1 and AccChr24, while LG24 and LG25 were linked together into AccChr25.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genomic basis of fiber production in CB5 pineapple</h3><p>Both F153 and CB5 have eight <i>CesA</i> genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">8</a>), grouped into those required for primary (<i>CesA1, 3, 6</i> and <i>9</i>) and secondary (<i>CesA4, 7, 8</i> and <i>11</i>) cell wall biosynthesis. The CB5 and F153 genomes share the same genes, but do not have orthologs of the <i>CesA2</i>, <i>5</i> and <i>10</i> genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">5</a>). In F153 and CB5, genes for primary cell wall biosynthesis were all highly expressed in leaves, flowers and fruit, except for <i>CesA9</i>. Interestingly, the <i>CesA4</i>, <i>7</i> and <i>8</i>, genes that are involved in secondary cell wall synthesis, were highly expressed in leaves of F153, while their expression levels were low in CB5 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>Lignin is the second major component of PALF. The full set of pineapple lignin biosynthetic genes were identified by sequence alignment to known <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice and poplar lignin synthesis pathway genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hamberger, B. et al. Genome-wide analyses of phenylpropanoid-related genes in Populus trichocarpa, Arabidopsis thaliana, and Oryza sativa: the Populus lignin toolbox and conservation and diversification of angiosperm gene families. Can. J. Bot. 85, 1182–1201 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1530">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ehlting, J. et al. Global transcript profiling of primary stems from Arabidopsis thaliana identifies candidate genes for missing links in lignin biosynthesis and transcriptional regulators of fiber differentiation. Plant J. 42, 618–640 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1533">21</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM3">1</a>). CB5 and F153 had 24 and 21 candidate genes for lignin biosynthesis, respectively. Three <i>PAL</i> genes in CB5 had higher expression in leaves than in F153 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">7</a>). Both <i>COMT1</i> and <i>CCOMT1</i> showed higher expression in CB5 than in F153 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Anthocyanin biosynthetic genes</h3><p>The variety <i>bracteatus</i> is often grown as an ornamental plant, partly because of the red color of its fruit. Anthocyanin biosynthesis shares the phenylpropanoid pathway with lignin biosynthesis in its first steps. Anthocyanin biosynthetic genes were identified in F153 and CB5 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">9</a>). The size of the CB5 gene families encoding anthocyanin biosynthetic genes was larger than in F153 (22 versus 17). Early biosynthetic genes in the pathway such as <i>CHS</i>, <i>CHI</i>, <i>F3H</i> and <i>F3</i>′<i>H</i> were expanded in CB5. Both F153 and CB5 did not have <i>FLS</i> and <i>ANS</i> orthologs, indicating the existence of their isozyme genes, which may take over their functions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Sugar metabolism genes</h3><p>Sweetness is a major fruit quality trait. In pineapple fruit, sucrose is the main sugar followed by glucose and fructose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Adisak, J. &amp; Jintana, J. in VII International Pineapple Symposium Vol. 902 (eds Abdullah, H. et al.) 423–426 (International Society for Horticultural Science, 2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1601">22</a></sup>. Multiple enzymes participate in their biosynthesis, transportation and metabolism with no difference in gene number between CB5 and fruit pineapple, including sucrose-phosphate synthases, sucrose-phosphate phosphatases, sucrose synthases, invertases, sucrose transporters (SUTs), sugars-will-eventually-be-exported transporters (SWEETs) and monosaccharide transporters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiang, S. Y. et al. Sucrose metabolism gene families and their biological functions. Sci. Rep. 5, 17583 (2015)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1605">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ruan, Y. L. Sucrose metabolism: gateway to diverse carbon use and sugar signaling. Ann. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 33–67 (2014)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1605_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Büttner, M. The monosaccharide transporter(-like) gene family in Arabidopsis. FEBS Letters 581, 2318–2324 (2007)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1605_2">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Doidy, J. et al. Sugar transporters in plants and in their interactions with fungi. Trends Plant Sci. 17, 413–422 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1608">26</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">10</a>). In CB5, SUTs were constantly expressed at a low level during fruit maturation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">11</a>), while two of SUT genes (<i>AccSUT1</i> and <i>AccSUT3</i>) were highly expressed in mature fruit in MD2 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">12</a>). More SWEET genes were expressed in the late developmental stage of fruit in MD2 than in CB5 (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">14</a>). More interestingly, <i>AccSWEET13</i> was located in the region of the F153 genome where a selective sweep was detected (see below). These results partially explain why MD2 accumulates more sugar in its fruit than CB5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Bromelains</h3><p>We identified 61 and 47 cysteine proteinase (CP)-type bromelains in F153 and CB5, respectively. Meanwhile, we identified 28 CPs in <i>Amborella</i>, 36 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, 34 in papaya, 25 in grape, 50 in poplar, 47 in sorghum and 50 in rice (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">15</a>). These <i>CP</i>s are divided into nine subfamilies (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">9</a>). Subfamily VI had the most members, while subfamilies V, VIII and IX had fewer members, with no more than three members in each species. An expansion was observed in subfamily VI in all the selected species, especially F153. Bromelains of pineapple belong to this subfamily, and the expansion may result in a high production of bromelains. The majority of CPs showed constant expression patterns during fruit ripening (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">17</a>). Some genes such as <i>AccCEP3</i> and <i>AccPAP25</i> showed dynamic expression patterns at a high level during the mature stage of fruit ripening (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">16</a>). In subfamily VI of CB5, only two genes displayed expression in the tissues studied. <i>AcbPAP10</i> was found to be expressed in flowers, fruit and leaves, while <i>AcbPAP17</i> was only expressed in flowers. More highly expressed genes in subfamily VI were detected in F153. <i>AccPAP3</i> and <i>AccPAP4</i> exhibited very high expression at late stages of fruit development, perhaps contributing to fruit ripening.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Patterns of genome-wide variation in pineapple</h3><p>We selected 89 <i>Ananas</i> accessions for whole-genome resequencing, including 67 accessions of <i>A. comosus</i> var. <i>comosus</i>, nine accessions of var. <i>bracteatus</i>, two accessions of var. <i>erectifolius</i>, nine accessions of the wild var. <i>microstachys</i>, and two accessions from <i>Pitcairnia gracilis</i> and <i>P. punicea</i> as outgroups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">18</a>). The var. <i>comosus</i> samples include representatives of the three historical cultivars ‘Queen’, ‘Smooth Cayenne’, and ‘Singapore Spanish’, associated with the pantropical diffusion of the pineapple in historical times. Other important var. <i>comosus</i> cultivars analyzed include ‘Pérola’ and several cultivars from north-western South America and Central America, as well as admixed breeding lines and cultivars of unknown origin. We also included cultivated clones of <i>A. comosous</i> var. <i>bracteatus</i>, and the proposed wild progenitor of pineapple <i>A. comosus</i> var. <i>microstachys</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G., Sanewski, G. M., Smith, M. K., Duval, M.-F. &amp; Leal, F. in Wild Crop Relatives: Genomic and Breeding Resources (ed. Kole C.) 21–41 (Springer, 2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1746">27</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">19</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Original collection sites of samples of A. comosus botanical varieties and cultivars used in the present study."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Original collection sites of samples of <i>A. comosus</i> botanical varieties and cultivars used in the present study.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="524" /></picture></a><p class="c-article-section__figure-credit text-right c-article-section__figure-credit-right" data-test="figure-credit">Image adapted from <a href="https://www.mapbox.com/about/maps/">Mapbox</a> and <a href="https://www.openstreetmap.org/copyright">OpenStreetMap</a></p></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Accessions are represented by the same color code as in the admixture analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3</a>). Accessions presenting less than 10% admixture are represented by the color of their cluster. For admixed accessions, the color that comes first in importance in the admixture diagram is indicated in the wider concentric circle, and the second in importance in the smaller concentric circle: light blue, Smooth Cayenne cluster of var. <i>comosus</i>; dark blue, Queen cluster; violet, Singapore Spanish cluster; yellow, fourth cluster (Mordilona-related cultivars); red, var. <i>erectifolius</i> cluster; orange, var. <i>bracteatus</i> cluster; green, var. <i>microstachys</i> cluster. Cultivars bearing the ‘piping’ spine-suppressor gene are identified by a red P-letter.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified 7,428,400 high-quality SNPs and &lt;10-base pair (bp) insertions/deletions (indels) across the 89 accessions. Cultivated pineapple yielded 3.2 million variants, including a large number of rare alleles (1.6 million with &lt;5% minor allele frequency). This high proportion of rare alleles was probably a product of unique somatic mutations expected with clonal propagation. Nearly half (3,526,071, 47.5 %) of the SNPs were located in intergenic regions. The proportions of SNPs from genic regions assigned to exon, intron and UTR regions were 17.1%, 31.8% and 3.6% respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">10</a>). A total of 12,806 SNPs with predicted effects on gene functions, such as altering start codons, stop codons or splice sites, were discovered. Overall, 7,084 SNPs introduced stop codons, 725 SNPs disrupted stop codons, 750 SNPs disrupted start codons and 4,252 SNPs affected splicing donor or acceptor sites (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">20</a>). With regards to the SNPs located in the exon regions, the number of nonsynonymous SNPs is less than synonymous SNPs for each accession (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">21</a>).</p><p>The nonsynonymous and synonymous site frequency spectra were examined for cultivars Smooth Cayenne, Queen and Singapore Spanish (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">11</a>). Smooth Cayenne has an excess of low-frequency nonsynonymous variants compared to synonymous variants, indicating purifying selection. For Queen and Singapore Spanish, there was an unusual excess of variants at an intermediate frequency for both nonsynonymous and synonymous sites. This was probably because Queen and Singapore Spanish had a higher abundance of heterozygous genotypes per SNP position (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Origin, population structure and genomic ancestry of pineapple</h3><p>We used a subset of 665,162 quality-filtered SNPs to explore relationships between the genomes of divergent <i>Ananas</i> taxa and cultivars. Phylogenetic trees and networks estimated with RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1844">28</a></sup> and SplitsTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huson, D. H. SplitsTree: analyzing and visualizing evolutionary data. Bioinformatics 14, 68–73 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1848">29</a></sup> separated accessions of the varieties <i>microstachys</i>, <i>bracteatus</i>, <i>erectifolius</i> and <i>comosus</i>, and accessions from major cultivars within the latter. Seven mislabeled cultivars were corrected, and six cultivars were assigned to correct cultivars that could not be classified previously (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">18</a>). For Singapore Spanish and Selangor Green, we confirmed and completed the history of their diffusion from Eastern Brazil to Asia. Similarly, the two var. <i>erectifolius</i> accessions were obtained from the same original collection through vegetative propagation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">18</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Pineapple population structure and admixture."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Pineapple population structure and admixture.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="554" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, SplitsTree network for <i>Ananas</i> accessions excluding admixed samples. Green, variety <i>microstachys</i>; red, variety <i>erectifolius</i>; orange, variety <i>bracteatus</i>; yellow, variety <i>comosus</i>/Mordilona-related cultivars Cambray/Monte Lirio; purple, variety <i>comosus</i>/cultivar Singapore Spanish; light blue, variety <i>comosus</i>/cultivar Smooth Cayenne; dark blue, variety <i>comosus</i>/cultivar Queen; brown, genus <i>Pitcairnia</i> outgroups. A network of admixed samples is shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">14</a>. <b>b</b>, MDS graphs of the studied <i>Ananas</i> accessions, with horizontal and vertical axes explaining 33.0% and 20.6% of the variance, respectively. Color code follows that in <b>a</b> and admixed <i>A. comosus</i> genotypes are indicated in gray. <b>c</b>, Ancestry results from ADMIXTURE under the <i>K</i> = 8 model supported by an examination of cross-validation errors (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">15</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To reduce the overrepresentation of Smooth Cayenne, Queen and Singapore Spanish/Selangor Green from population structure analyses, only five accessions were retained for each of the corresponding clusters (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3</a>). Within variety <i>comosus</i>, the three groups that corresponded to the major cultivars Smooth Cayenne, Queen Singapore Spanish and a few cultivars derived from them, formed three clusters whose variation essentially originated from somatic mutations accumulated during the two to five centuries after their diffusion out of America. Smooth Cayenne and Queen dispersed from the Guianas, while Singapore Spanish and Selangor Green dispersed from the eastern coast of Brazil (south of Bahia)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G., Duval, M.-F., Leal, F. in Genetics and Genomics of Pineapple (ed. Ming, R.) 1–25 (Springer, 2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d47403e1968">30</a></sup>. Common cultivars of <i>A. comosus</i> exhibit greatly reduced diversity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">16</a>), consistent with genetic bottlenecks from domestication. Nucleotide diversity was reduced more than 15 times in pineapple cultivars compared to their wild <i>A. comosus</i> var. <i>microstachys</i> progenitor (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">16</a>), which is consistent with reduced diversity seen in multidimensional scaling (MDS) space and phylogenetic branch lengths (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">13</a>) and high population differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) among major cultivars (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">16</a>). The typical accessions of var. <i>bracteatus</i> also formed a uniform group, where variation appeared to be related to somatic mutations; however, five less typical accessions showed admixture with cultivars of var. <i>comosus</i>. At a greater genealogical depth, composite likelihood estimation with TreeMix detected a predicted admixture event between var. <i>bracteatus</i> and its <i>A. macrodontes</i> parent (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><p>The diversity and relatedness patterns were confirmed by MDS of genomic data (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">19</a>). SplitsTree branch lengths involving the varieties <i>microstachys</i>, <i>bracteatus</i>, <i>erectifolius</i> and <i>comosus</i> were compared to those between major cultivars of <i>comosus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3a</a>). Absolute genomic divergence (<i>D</i><sub>xy</sub>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">16</a>) was significantly greater among pairs of varieties, compared to major cultivars of <i>comosus</i> (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.005). <i>D</i><sub>xy</sub> among <i>Ananas</i> varieties was on average 0.0059 (median 0.0046, s.e.m. 0.0007), which is within the range of expectations for recently derived species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Chapman, M. A., Hiscock, S. J. &amp; Filatov, D. A. Genomic divergence during speciation driven by adaptation to altitude. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2553–2567 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2073">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Cruickshank, T. E. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Reanalysis suggests that genomic islands of speciation are due to reduced diversity, not reduced gene flow. Mol. Ecol. 23, 3133–3157 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2076">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Local genetic ancestry of hybrid accessions estimated with a Hidden Markov Model approach revealed a great diversity of patterns, including hybrids with large ancestry segments stemming from different modern <i>comosus</i> cultivars, and hybrids with small ancestry segments from different cultivars and taxa (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig4">4</a>). The presence of both large and small segments in hybrids indicated that admixture has affected the evolution of variety <i>comosus</i> over long time scales. Our most likely models of local ancestry were consistent with an average of 37 generations since the onset of admixture (range, 21–55) among the 22 var. <i>comosus</i> hybrids detected in our study. For the wild variety <i>microstachys</i>, individual estimates range from 107 to 612 generations, respectively. These numbers probably translated into several thousand years as perennial, primarily asexually propagated, plants.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Summary plots of average local ancestry across all comosus admixed samples for chromosomes 3, 4 and 5."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Summary plots of average local ancestry across all <i>comosus</i> admixed samples for chromosomes 3, 4 and 5.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="389" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Genomic regions with unusually high ancestry proportions from particular pineapple varieties are visible along chromosomes. The remaining chromosomes show similar results. For color coding labels see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3</a>. All ancestry values sum to 1 for each genomic window.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genomic signatures of mitotic selection and clonal propagation</h3><p>Somatic mutation is a major driving force that shapes the domestication and diversification of clonally propagated plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Eckert, C. G. in Ecology and Evolutionary Biology of Clonal Plants (eds Stuefer, J.F. et al.) 279–298 (Springer, 2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2134">33</a></sup>. One source of somatic mutation is the movement of transposable elements (TEs). We surveyed the presence/absence of variation of small DNA TEs in 89 resequenced accessions. DNA TEs were highly abundant, attaining copy numbers up to tens of thousands<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chen, J., Hu, Q., Zhang, Y., Lu, C. &amp; Kuang, H. P-MITE: a database for plant miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1176–D1181 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2138">34</a></sup> and they predominantly insert into or near gene-rich regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wessler, S. R., Bureau, T. E. &amp; White, S. E. LTR-retrotransposons and MITEs: important players in the evolution of plant genomes. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 5, 814–821 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2142">35</a></sup>. MITE-Hunter software predicted 4,614 TE junctions consisting of 2,286 <i>Mutator</i>, 1,156 hAT, 1,018 PIF/Harbinger, 128 CACTA and 26 unknown elements. The unique junction sites created by TE insertions were used as a reference for read mapping to assess the presence/absence of variation against the F153 reference. In total, 98,476 TE junctions were identified in the reference pineapple assembly: 46,613 <i>Mutator</i>, 23,634 PIF/Harbinger, 18,831 hAT, 4,091 CACTA, 254 unknowns and 12 junctions formed by two different TE superfamilies. Compared to the F153 reference genome, each accession exhibited a great number of unique TE junctions, which varied from 97% identity with F153 in Ac50 to 28% identity with F153 in Ac46c (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">22</a>). The high variability of TE insertion sites in pineapple might be a driver for new traits via somatic mutation during domestication.</p><p>The process of mitotic recombination was predicted to lead to terminal homozygosity over time in tissues or organisms propagated exclusively through somatic means. This random generation of homozygosity in initially heterozygous tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stern, C. Somatic crossing over and segregation in Drosophila melanogaster. Genetics 21, 625 (1936)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2159">36</a></sup> could provide selectable genetic variation by uncovering recessive alleles. Hence, we investigated this question by first finding all of the single-copy (SC) genes in the pineapple genome. SC genes were chosen so that identification of heterozygosity versus homozygosity for any given chromosomal location could be ascertained without the confusion generated by paralogs. The final 10,439 SC genes were distributed randomly across the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">20</a>).</p><p>Terminal runs of homozygosity at the ends of LGs were frequently detected, especially in Singapore Spanish, including LG01, 03, 04, 08, 11, 14, 15, 20, 22 and 24 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig5">5</a>, and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">21</a>). Some of this homozygosity covered the entire region, from the site of the mitotic recombination to the end of the chromosome, as expected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="LaFave, M. C. &amp; Sekelsky, J. Mitotic recombination: why? when? how? where? PLoS Genet. 5, e1000411 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2175">37</a></sup>. The presence of such terminal runs of homozygosity indicated an early occurrence (and possible selection and fixation) of associated mitotic mutations in the domestication process. In Smooth Cayenne and Queen, short terminal homozygosity was detected sporadically in LG03 and 23, and was likely to be a product of mixed clonal and sexual reproduction. Notably, the overall level of heterozygosity (and the lack of all but a tiny number of homozygous regions) in the wild relatives of pineapple indicated that these populations were prodigious outcrossers.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Long tracks of terminal homozygosity in the Singapore Spanish pineapple cultivar."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Long tracks of terminal homozygosity in the Singapore Spanish pineapple cultivar.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="323" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Levels of heterozygosity were plotted for every 100 bp across linearly concatenated genes and across 50 accessions with low levels of admixture. A heatmap of heterozygosity is plotted where white indicates no heterozygosity and dark purple indicates high heterozygosity. The vertical dotted red line indicates predicted centromere region as determined by Gypsy LTR retrotransposon abundance. The dendrograms on the left indicate clustered heterozygosity landscapes among varieties.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Selective sweeps and selection on sexually derived forms during pineapple domestication</h3><p>Genomic regions of selection during pineapple domestication were identified based on drastic reductions in nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) in cultivated accessions compared to wild lines (<i>π</i><sub>c</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>w</sub>) in sliding windows across the genome. Diversity within variety <i>microstachys</i> was used for estimating <i>π</i><sub>w.</sub> Cultivars with evidence of admixture were omitted from selection scans and <i>π</i><sub>c</sub> was calculated within and across each of the four cultivars. Candidate swept regions were further narrowed using an cross-population composite likelihood ratio test (XP-CLR) based approach to model the allele frequency spectrum differences between cultivated and wild accessions. This approach identified 25 putative domestication sweeps across the pineapple genome with sizes ranging from 150 kb to 1.2 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">23</a>). Swept regions collectively spanned 11.9 Mb (~3.1% of the genome) which was substantially lower than patterns observed in sexually propagated crops such as tomato (186 domestication sweeps totaling 64.5 Mb, ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. 46, 1220–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2232">38</a></sup>) and soybean (121 sweeps totaling 53 Mb, ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhou, Z. et al. Resequencing 302 wild and cultivated accessions identifies genes related to domestication and improvement in soybean. Nature Biotechnology 33, 408–414 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2236">39</a></sup>). Pineapple also had fewer putative selective sweeps than other clonally propagated crops such as cassava, which contains signatures of 224 sweeps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ramu, P. et al. Cassava HapMap: Managing genetic load in a clonal crop species. Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/077123&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2241">40</a></sup>.</p><p>Swept regions in pineapple encompassed 392 genes with enrichment in stress response pathways (FDR = 2.1 × 10<sup>−3</sup>), but no obvious enrichment in genes previously characterized in other species as responsible for domestication-related traits. To narrow this list of candidate domestication genes, we surveyed gene expression changes in a high-resolution series of developing pineapple fruit. The strongest sweep was a 225 kb region at the beginning of LG03 with a 400-fold reduction in diversity across cultivated accessions compared to the wild var. <i>microstachys</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig6">6a</a>). This sweep was in the top 5% based on XP-CLR that indicated low <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> and highly negative Tajima’s <i>D</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig6">6b,c</a>). Although the sweep on LG03 overlaps with a long run of terminal homozygosity (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">21.3</a>), it was much narrower than the homozygosity run (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig5">5</a>). The putative sweep contains nine genes, including a pair of tandemly duplicated bromelain inhibitors (<i>AccBI1</i> and <i>AccBI2</i>) with fruit-specific expression patterns (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig6">6d</a>). Bromelains coordinating with bromelain inhibitors are supposed to play an important role in pineapple fruit ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Neuteboom, L. W., Matsumoto, K. O. &amp; Christopher, D. A. An extended AE-rich N-terminal trunk in secreted pineapple cystatin enhances inhibition of fruit bromelain and is posttranslationally removed during ripening. Plant Physiol. 151, 515–527 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2283">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Raimbault, A. K., Zuily-Fodil, Y., Soler, A., Mora, P. &amp; de Carvalho, M. H. C. The expression patterns of bromelain and AcCYS1 correlate with blackheart resistance in pineapple fruits submitted to postharvest chilling stress. J. Plant Physiol. 170, 1442–1446 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2286">42</a></sup>. Bromelain inhibitor is posttranslationally inactivated during fruit ripening, leading to a significant increase in bromelain activity, thus enhancing tissue proteolysis, softening and degradation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Neuteboom, L. W., Matsumoto, K. O. &amp; Christopher, D. A. An extended AE-rich N-terminal trunk in secreted pineapple cystatin enhances inhibition of fruit bromelain and is posttranslationally removed during ripening. Plant Physiol. 151, 515–527 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2290">41</a></sup>. <i>AccBI1</i> and <i>2</i> are the most highly expressed genes during fruit ripening, with reads per kilobase per million mapped reads (RPKMs) as high as 443,814. Expression of <i>AccBI1</i> oscillates down to 0 RPKMs in some ripening stages, suggesting strict transcriptional control. Pineapple F153 contained 61 bromelain genes, including two that have expression patterns that correlate inversely with <i>AccBI1</i> and <i>AccBI2</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig6">6d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Putative domestication sweep around a bromelain inhibitor gene that helps control fruit ripening."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Putative domestication sweep around a bromelain inhibitor gene that helps control fruit ripening.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0506-8/MediaObjects/41588_2019_506_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="558" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Top: Heat maps showing the distribution of domestication sweeps (top 5% <i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub>) for the four cultivars. Bottom: A putative swept region at the end of LG03 containing <i>AccBI1</i> and <i>AccBI2</i>. <i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub> across all cultivars is plotted using a sliding window of 0.5 Mb with 0.1 Mb shift. <b>b</b>, Genetic distance (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) between the Smooth Cayenne, Queen, Singapore Spanish and Mordilona-related clusters for the 6.5 Mb of LG03. Mean <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values are plotted in sliding windows of 50 kb with 25 kb step size. <b>c</b>, Tajima’s <i>D</i> values for the four combined cultivar clusters (var. <i>comosus</i>) and wild (var. <i>microstachys</i>). Mean Tajima’s <i>D</i> values are plotted in sliding windows of 50 kb with a step size of 25 kb. <b>d</b>, Left: Pineapple fruit at select stages from a fruit ripening series (stages 1, 2, 7 and 8). Right: Maximum likelihood phylogeny of bromelain genes with log<sub>2</sub> transformed RPKMs of expression in fruit, flower, leaf and root tissue.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Candidate genes for self-incompatibility in pineapple</h3><p>In contrast to <i>A. macrodontes</i>, <i>A. comosus</i> and its botanical varieties are self-incompatible, with exception of some clones of var. <i>bracteatus</i>. However, self-incompatibility tends to be stronger in var. <i>comosus</i>, compared to the other varieties that were not domesticated for fruit, which is probably a result of selection under domestication to reduce seed set in fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G., Duval, M.-F. &amp; Van Miegroet, F. Fertility and self-incompatibility in the genus Ananas. Acta Hortic. 334, 45–52 (1992)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2412">43</a></sup>. Gametophytic self-incompatibility (GSI) operated in cultivated pineapple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Brewbaker, J. L. &amp; Gorrez, D. D. Genetics of self-incompatibility in the monocot genera, Ananas (pineapple) and Gasteria. Am. J. Bot. 54, 611–616 (1967)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2417">44</a></sup>, similar to S-RNase-based GSI, in which the S-locus encodes a single S-RNase and multiple S-locus F-box (SLFs/SFBs) proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bedinger, P. A., Broz, A. K., Tovar-Mendez, A. &amp; McClure, B. Pollen-pistil interactions and their role in mate selection. Plant Physiol. 173, 79–90 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2421">45</a></sup>. When self-pollinated in SI species, none of the SLFs/SFBs interact with their own S-RNase, which breaks down pollen tube RNA to inhibit growth; when cross-pollinated, some members of paternal SLFs/SFBs interact with maternal S-RNase, which allows pollen tube growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bedinger, P. A., Broz, A. K., Tovar-Mendez, A. &amp; McClure, B. Pollen-pistil interactions and their role in mate selection. Plant Physiol. 173, 79–90 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2425">45</a></sup>. To search for genes potentially involved in pineapple GSI, we first identified S-RNase and SLF/SFB homologs in the pineapple reference genome based on sequence homology. These candidates were then tested for their selection history in diverse pineapple varieties. Twenty-five genes passed the criteria (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">24</a>).</p><p>We examined the transcript levels of the 25 SI candidate genes in androecium and gynoecium, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">24</a>). Two S<i>-</i>RNase genes (Aco001100 and Aco004758), the potential female specificity determinants in GSI, were highly expressed in both tissues but with stronger expression in androecium. For the SLFs/SFBs, two of the genes (Aco00868 and Aco011265) showed much stronger expression in androecium than gynoecium, while two of them (Aco015095 and Aco021447) showed the opposite expression bias. Expression of four genes was not detected. The remaining 13 genes showed similar expression in both tissues. Among the six genes showing differential expression in androecium and gynoecium, the ribonuclease T2 family member Aco001100 and F-box family member Aco00868 are tightly linked on LG02, only 1.8 Mb apart, and they are the most likely candidates for self-incompatibility in <i>A. comosus</i> var. <i>comosus</i> Smooth Cayenne F153. Furthermore, Aco001100 was tightly linked with two other SLF/SFB genes (Aco001170 and Aco012216), a characteristic of RNase-based GSI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bedinger, P. A., Broz, A. K., Tovar-Mendez, A. &amp; McClure, B. Pollen-pistil interactions and their role in mate selection. Plant Physiol. 173, 79–90 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2447">45</a></sup>. In CB5, the ribonuclease T2 family member CB5.v30014510 was the orthologous gene of Aco001100 and linked with only one SLF/SFB family gene (CB5.v30013780), which is not a functional SI system (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">25</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>The chromosomal-level assembly of the <i>bracteatus</i> pineapple CB5 genome sheds more light on the biology and evolution of <i>Ananas</i>. To overcome the problem of assembling a heterozygous genome, we have developed an algorithm, Pseudohaploid, that identifies and filters out heterozygous contigs by searching for redundant homologous sequences. Facilitated by long-read sequencing technology, we identified and located more repetitive sequences in the CB5 genome, providing comprehensive resources to study genome evolution driven by TEs. In addition, the misassembled pseudo-chromosomes in the F153 genome were corrected with the assistance of the CB5 genome. Comparison between these two pineapple genomes revealed genomic components associated with fiber production, color formation, sugar accumulation and fruit maturation. It also provided an additional line of evidence to verify SI candidate genes in F153.</p><p>Our genomic data indicated the presence of a continuum of divergence, ranging from low divergence among groups of modern pineapple cultivars to moderate divergence among closely related taxa, such as the cultivated botanical varieties <i>comosus</i>, <i>bracteatus</i> and <i>erectifolius</i>, to a much greater divergence in the wild var. <i>microstachys</i>, which exhibits <i>D</i><sub>xy</sub> values normally seen among recently diverged species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chen, J., Hu, Q., Zhang, Y., Lu, C. &amp; Kuang, H. P-MITE: a database for plant miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1176–D1181 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2490">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wessler, S. R., Bureau, T. E. &amp; White, S. E. LTR-retrotransposons and MITEs: important players in the evolution of plant genomes. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 5, 814–821 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2493">35</a></sup>. In contrast, <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> reflected low diversity in major cultivars, consistent with the domestication bottleneck. Greatly reduced diversity in cultivars relative to their wild progenitor pointed to the severe domestication bottleneck experienced by this clonally propagated crop, and an excess of intermediate frequency alleles in two major groups of modern cultivars indicated the potential for clonal propagation to mask recessive deleterious variants in heterozygotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Gaut, B. S., Seymour, D. K., Liu, Q. P. &amp; Zhou, Y. F. Demography and its effects on genomic variation in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 4, 512–520 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2501">46</a></sup>.</p><p>Admixture analysis of <i>A. comosus</i> cultivars revealed widespread admixture genotypes in 39 (44%) out of 89 accessions, detected in every cultivar and botanical variety. With regard to evolutionary processes operating during pineapple domestication, our results indicated a role for both ancient and recent admixture and thus sexual recombination and subsequent artificial selection in most cultivars. This was supported by the dearth of terminal runs of homozygosity along the chromosomes of pineapple in two out of three major cultivars. This indicated that both sexual recombination and somatic mutations have contributed to the phenotypic diversity seen in <i>Ananas</i>. It appears that the true degree of genomic complexity of germplasm used in 20th-century breeding programs was previously underestimated.</p><p>Early pre-Columbian pineapple cultivars were selected for low fruit fiber content and reduced seed production through lower fertility and self-incompatibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Coppens d’Eeckenbrugge, G., Leal, F. &amp; Bartholomew, D. in The Pineapple: Botany, Production and Uses 13–32 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2517">47</a></sup>. The pineapple genome contains 25 selective sweeps, much fewer than those in sexually reproducing crops such as the 121 in soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhou, Z. et al. Resequencing 302 wild and cultivated accessions identifies genes related to domestication and improvement in soybean.
