Nature Biotechnology 33, 408–414 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2521">39</a></sup> and the 186 in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. 46, 1220–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2525">38</a></sup>, supporting the conclusion of a mixture of sexual and asexual selection for pineapple. The strongest selective sweep included a pair of tandemly duplicated bromelain inhibitors previously suggested as important regulators of pineapple fruit senescence and ripening in this nonclimacteric fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Neuteboom, L. W., Matsumoto, K. O. &amp; Christopher, D. A. An extended AE-rich N-terminal trunk in secreted pineapple cystatin enhances inhibition of fruit bromelain and is posttranslationally removed during ripening. Plant Physiol. 151, 515–527 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2529">41</a></sup>. Gene duplications are the drivers of evolutionary innovation and have been linked to domestication traits in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Xiao, H., Jiang, N., Schaffner, E., Stockinger, E. J. &amp; van der Knaap, E. A retrotransposon-mediated gene duplication underlies morphological variation of tomato fruit. Science 319, 1527–1530 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2533">48</a></sup> and black raspberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="VanBuren, R. et al. The genome of black raspberry (Rubus occidentalis). Plant J. 87, 535–547 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2538">49</a></sup>. The bromelain inhibitor gene duplication event was probably selected in pre-Columbian varieties.</p><p>Our initial working hypothesis was that somatic mutations were the main source of variation for domestication in pineapple. Our efforts to identify mitotic selective sweeps were fruitful in the cultivar Singapore Spanish as shown by extensive terminal runs of homozygosity, the hallmark of mitotic selection. However, this hypothesis was rejected for two major cultivars, Smooth Cayenne and Queen, although sporadic terminal runs were detected in two chromosomes, indicating long term clonal reproduction punctuated by sexual reproductions. Meiosis in pineapple generally occurs once every 2 years, while recombination in mitotic cells is continuous but at very low rates, about 10<sup>4</sup> to 10<sup>5</sup> times less frequent than meiotic recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Paques, F. &amp; Haber, J. E. Multiple pathways of recombination induced by double-strand breaks in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Microbiol. Mol. Biol. Rev. 63, 349–404 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2550">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Petes T. D. &amp; Symington L. S. in The Molecular and Cellular Biology of the Yeast Saccharomyces (eds Jones, E.W. et al.) 407–521 (Cold Spring Harbor Press, 1991)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2553">51</a></sup>. At such a low frequency and the nature of clonal production, only mitotic recombination events that occurred at the single cell stage of the reproductive tissues, crowns, suckers, slips and shoots, could be transmitted to progenies and preserved to be detectable. Moreover, one sexual recombination could interrupt terminal runs of homology that had formed and been maintained over thousands of years.</p><p>The one-step operation hypothesis, wherein domestication and early improvement are an immediate outcome of a single clonal propagant might be responsible for the selection of some long-lasting clones in some lineages. Genomic analyses, particularly those searching for terminal runs of homozygosity, can be applied to other clonally propagated crops to elucidate the extent of sexual recombination versus vegetative descent in their domestication history. The coexistence of sexual recombination and the one-step operation among different cultivars might be common in clonally propagated crops. Some controversial hypotheses were rejected in the past, but later validated entirely or partly by innovative new technologies or enhanced resolution of evidence, including the ‘dominance’ versus ‘overdominance’ hypotheses for heterosis and Lamarck’s theory of the inheritance of acquired characteristics. The hypothesis of the one-step operation for the domestication of clonally propagated crops thus seems to be one of them.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome assembly and annotation overview</h3><p>The CB5 chromosome level assembly takes advantage of PacBio Single-Molecule Real-Time technology and Hi-C based scaffolding methods, followed by Illumina short read-based polishing. Briefly, ~50× coverage of subreads were generated with the PacBio RSII platform and ~60× coverage by short reads was generated on the Illumina HiSeq X10 platform. The initial contig-level assembly was accomplished with CANU v.1.7 and heterozygous contigs were removed using our newly developed algorithm, Pseudohaploid (details in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Further, Illumina short reads were recruited to polish the PacBio assembled genome using Pilon v.1.18 with parameters: —diploid —threads 6 —changes —tracks —fix bases —verbose —mindepth 4. Hi-C libraries were created from tender leaves of CB5 at BioMarker Technologies Corporation as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Xie, T. et al. De novo plant genome assembly based on chromatin interactions: a case study of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Plant 8, 489–492 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2575">52</a></sup>. Chimeric fragments representing the original cross-linked fragments were then processed into paired-end sequencing libraries and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq X10 platform. The paired-end reads were uniquely mapped onto the draft assembly and misjoined contigs were corrected by detecting abrupt long-range contact patterns using the 3D-DNA pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2579">53</a></sup>. The Hi-C corrected contigs were further linked into 25 pseudo-chromosomes using the ALLHiC pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Zhang, J. et al. Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L. Nat. Genet. 50, 1565–1573 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2583">54</a></sup>.</p><p>We annotated the CB5 chromosomal-level assembly using a series of programs, which are fully described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>. Briefly, the MAKER2 pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Campbell, M. S., Holt, C., Moore, B. &amp; Yandell, M. Genome Annotation and Curation Using MAKER and MAKER-P. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 48, 11–39 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2593">55</a></sup> was used to annotate the protein-coding proteins by integrating homologous proteins, RNA-seq assembled transcripts and the results of ab initio gene predictors. In addition, repetitive sequences were predicted by RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0 &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013–2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2597">56</a></sup> and we also predicted miRNAs by searching for candidates that matched to public miRNAs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Identification of lignin and anthocyanin biosynthetic genes</h3><p>Protein sequences for lignin biosynthetic genes of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hamberger, B. et al. Genome-wide analyses of phenylpropanoid-related genes in Populus trichocarpa, Arabidopsis thaliana, and Oryza sativa: the Populus lignin toolbox and conservation and diversification of angiosperm gene families. Can. J. Bot. 85, 1182–1201 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2617">20</a></sup> were used to align with the protein sequences of F153 and CB5 using BLASTP with a cut off <i>e</i> value ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> and coverage ≥ 0.75. For anthocyanin biosynthetic genes identification, protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Guo, N. et al. Anthocyanin biosynthetic genes in Brassica rapa. BMC Genomics 15, 426 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2629">57</a></sup> were used to align with the protein sequences of F153 and CB5. Pfam was adopted to identify conserved domains for these candidate genes. Finally, we used MEGA 7 to draw a phylogenetic tree to confirm the expected relatedness of anthocyanin biosynthetic genes. The phylogenetic tree was inferred using the neighbor-joining method. The alignment was done by MUSCLE v.3.8.31 with default substitution model and 1,000 bootstraps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Identification of CP subfamily C1 genes</h3><p>Gene models of all the species used in this study were downloaded from Phytozome v.11.0 and v.12.0 (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html</a>). The conserved domain of cysteine peptidase subfamily C1, peptidase_C1 domain (PF00112) was downloaded from the pfam database (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/">http://pfam.xfam.org/</a>). HMMER were used to search against protein databases for each species to identify proteins containing peptidase_C1 domain with threshold of <i>e</i> value ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>. We further confirmed those proteins by searching their domains against Conserved Domains Database from NCBI. Full-length proteins were aligned by MUSCLE v.3.8.31 with default parameters. Phylogenetic trees were constructed by Smart Model Selection PhyML v.3.0 with statistical criteria (AIC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Lefort, V., Longueville, J.-E. &amp; Gascuel, O. SMS: Smart model selection in PhyML. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 2422–2424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2660">58</a></sup> and were further edited with MEGA 7.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Variant calling and annotation</h3><p>A total of 4.7 billion 150–250 bp paired-end Illumina reads yielded an average coverage of 17.5× per accession (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM1">19</a>). This read depth is similar to other large-scale resequencing projects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhou, Z. et al. Resequencing 302 wild and cultivated accessions identifies genes related to domestication and improvement in soybean. Nature Biotechnology 33, 408–414 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2675">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. 6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2678">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Qi, J. et al. A genomic variation map provides insights into the genetic basis of cucumber domestication and diversity. Nat. Genet. 45, 1510–1515 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2681">60</a></sup>. Raw reads were quality-filtered to remove adapters and low-quality bases (Q &lt; 30). Quality-filtered reads were aligned against the unmasked F153 pineapple draft genome (v.6) using Bowtie2 (v.2.2.6) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2685">61</a></sup>) with default parameters. Read mapping rates for cultivated accessions ranged from 82.3% to 94.5% with an average of 87.6% compared to 69.4–84.2% for wild <i>Ananas</i> and related species. Variant detection was performed using the genome analysis toolkit (GATK; v.3.5-0-g36282e4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491–498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2692">62</a></sup> following the best practices workflow for variant discovery. Resulting BAM files were locally realigned using IndelRealigner to remove erroneous mismatches around small-scale insertions and deletions. Variants were called in each accession separately using HaplotypeCaller and individual genome Variant Call Format (gVCF) files were merged using GenotypeGVCFs. This two-step approach includes quality recalibration and regenotyping in the merged vcf file, ensuring variant accuracy. The flag –output_mode EMIT_ALL_CONFIDENT_SITES was used to provide read coverage for each position in the reference genome (including invariant sites), allowing regions with no alignment to be filtered out before population genetics analysis. A total of 9,342,943 raw variants were called by GATK. These variants were filtered to remove sites with quality scores less than 100, minimum allele frequency &lt;0.02, and missing data &gt;10%. The final vcf file contains 7,428,400 high-quality SNPs and indels (&lt;10 bp) across the 89 accessions. Variants were annotated using SNPEff (v.4.2) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strainw1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly (Austin). 6, 80–92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2697">63</a></sup>) with pineapple gene models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2701">19</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Nonsynonymous and synonymous site allele frequency analysis</h3><p>SNPEff annotated nonsynonymous and synonymous sites were used for site allele frequency analysis. Only SNPs from Smooth Cayenne, Queen and Singapore Spanish accessions were used because of their higher sample sizes. The <i>bracteatus</i> botanical variety was used as an outgroup to polarize ancestral and derived variants. Allele frequency was estimated separately for each population and SNP positions in more than 70% of each population’s sample size were analyzed. Because each SNP position had different sample sizes, we used the hypergeometric distribution to down-sample the <i>j</i>th SNP positions’ observed sample size, <i>N</i><sub><i>j</i></sub>, to the most minimum downsampled sample size across all SNP positions, <i>n</i> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Nielsen, R. et al. A scan for positively selected genes in the genomes of humans and chimpanzees. PLoS Biol. 3, e170 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2729">64</a></sup>). Thus, the allele frequency for a down-sample size of <i>n</i> was calculated as:</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${{p}}_{{{i}},2{{n}}} = k^{ - 1}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{j = 1}^k \frac{{\left( {\begin{array}{*{20}{c}} {d_j} \\ i \end{array}} \right)\left( {\begin{array}{*{20}{c}} {2{{N}}_j - {{d}}_{{j}}} \\ {2n - i} \end{array}} \right)}}{{\left( {\begin{array}{*{20}{c}} {2N_j} \\ {2n} \end{array}} \right)}}$$</span></div></div><p>where <i>p</i><sub><i>i,</i>2<i>n</i></sub> corresponds to the allele frequency of <i>i</i> derived alleles in a diploid 2<i>n</i> population, <i>d</i><sub><i>j</i></sub> is the observed derived allele count for site <i>j</i> and <i>k</i> is the total number of SNP positions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Linkage disequilibrium (LD) analysis</h3><p>The final vcf file was used for genome-wide LD calculation using individuals with nonadmixed evolutionary histories (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Fig3">3c</a>). Using PLINK (v.1.90b3.46) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559–575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d47403e2998">65</a></sup>), LD between SNP pairs within the same LG was calculated using a 5 Mb window and limiting to SNPs that were not more than 499,999 SNPs apart. SNPs within LGs that were at least 10 Mb in length were analyzed. SNP pairs were then grouped into 10 kb bins to average the R-squared correlation (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) between SNPs. SNP pairs with <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> values &lt;0.1 were omitted. The LOESS method of line of best fit was fitted using the average <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> value per bin.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">RNA-seq analysis</h3><p>The trimmed paired- or single-end reads of each sample were aligned to the repeat-masked F153 genome v.6 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3023">19</a></sup>), using TopHat (v.2.0.9) under default settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3027">66</a></sup>. The normalized RPKM value of each sample was estimated by Cufflinks v.2.2.1, followed by Cuffnorm v.2.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3031">66</a></sup>) using default settings with the pineapple gene model annotation (v.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3035">19</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Admixture, phylogenetics and population structure analyses</h3><p>SNPs from whole-genome resequencing were filtered using vcftools v.0.1.13 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3047">67</a></sup>) with minimum allelic count = 2, maximum missing data = 15%, minimum coverage = 4, SNP quality &gt; 20, retaining only biallelic variants and no indels. A maximum likelihood-based tree of <i>Ananas</i> accessions was built using RAxML v.8.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3054">28</a></sup>) with 100 bootstrap replicates to determine branch support, and a phylogenetic network was constructed using the neighbor-net method implemented within SplitsTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Huson, D. H. &amp; Bryant, D. Application of phylogenetic networks in evolutionary studies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 254–267 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3058">68</a></sup>. Additionally, MDS was used for model-free clustering of <i>Ananas</i> accessions. Nucleotide diversities (<i>π</i>), <i>D</i><sub>xy</sub> and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> were estimated for all taxa and major cultivars. Nucleotide diversities in wild and cultivated forms were used as a simple, robust approach to document genetic bottlenecks experienced during domestication; we refrained from demographic modeling of cultivar history using diffusion- or coalescent-based approaches due to the widespread presence of clonally propagated genotypes in the sample set, which would violate basic modeling assumptions. Instead, we explored key aspects of cultivar history by analyzing genomic patterns of ancestry. Genome-wide ancestry and admixture were estimated with ADMIXTURE v.1.23 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Alexander, D. H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3077">69</a></sup>). For variety <i>comosus</i> cultivars, this analysis used only the five samples with the highest coverage for each cultivar to avoid biases due to the overrepresentation of clonal samples. Population splits and past admixture events were further explored using the TreeMix approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Pickrell, J. K. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002967 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3085">70</a></sup>. Local ancestry along confidently assembled pineapple chromosomes was estimated with a Hidden Markov Model approach modified from Price et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Price, A. L. et al. Sensitive detection of chromosomal segments of distinct ancestry in admixed populations. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000519 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3089">71</a></sup> following Wegmann et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Wegmann, D. et al. Recombination rates in admixed individuals identified by ancestry-based inference. Nat. Genet. 43, 847–853 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3093">72</a></sup>, making use of the RASPberry software. The most likely number of generations since admixture was estimated for each admixed individual by this method based on likelihood ratio tests. Except where noted, statistical analyses were carried out in R.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Detecting putative selective sweeps</h3><p>Regions of selection during pineapple domestication were identified based on drastic reductions in <i>π</i> of cultivated accessions compared to wild lines (<i>π</i><sub>c</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>w</sub>) in sliding windows across the genome. Variety <i>microstachys</i> is the likely progenitor of cultivated pineapple, so diversity within this group was used for estimating <i>π</i><sub>w.</sub> Cultivars with evidence of admixture were omitted from selection scans and <i>π</i><sub>c</sub> was calculated within and across each of the four cultivars. To reduce false positives due to drift, the four cultivated groups were combined into a single pool before analysis. Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) was calculated using the –window-pi-step tool in vcftools (v.0.1.12) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3131">67</a></sup>). Invariant sites were included in calculations of π to remove any inflations in estimation related to missing data. Nucleotide diversity was calculated in sliding windows of 50 kb with a 10-kb step size to identify sweeps and in sliding windows of 10 kb with a 2.5 kb step to narrow candidate genes. The top 5% of <i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub> values were considered swept regions. Adjoining swept windows were merged into blocks, producing a final set of 25 swept regions.</p><p>Candidate swept regions were further narrowed using an XP-CLR based approach to model the allele frequency spectrum differences between cultivated and wild accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Chen, H., Patterson, N. &amp; Reich, D. Population differentiation as a test for selective sweeps. Genome Res. 20, 393–402 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3147">73</a></sup>. The following parameters were used for XP-CLR scans across each chromosome: window of 0.005 cM, window size of 1,000 bp, a maximum of 100 SNPs per grid, and a corrLevel of 0.7. The genetic distance between adjacent variants was calculated using the ultra high-density genetic map used to anchor the F153 pineapple draft genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435–1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3151">19</a></sup>. Comparisons were made between var. <i>comosus</i> cultivars showing no evidence of recent admixture and var. <i>microstachys</i>. Regions with the top 10% XP-CLR scores were merged as putative swept regions and only regions overlapping with high <i>π</i><sub>w</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>c</sub> values were kept to remove false positives.</p><p><i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> was estimated with the Weir and Cockerman approach using four-way comparisons of the cultivar clusters (Smooth Cayenne, Queen, Singapore Spanish and Mordilona-related) in the program SFselect (<a href="https://github.com/rronen/SFselect">https://github.com/rronen/SFselect</a>). Tajima’s <i>D</i> was calculated in sliding windows of 50 kb with 25 kb overlap using a suite of programs in vcftools (v.0.1.12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3186">67</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Identification and mapping of transposable element insertion sites</h3><p>MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3198">74</a></sup> was used with default parameters to search the pineapple genome assembly for candidate small DNA TEs. MITE-Hunter outputs were manually examined to select bona fide TEs based on their flanking sequences, TIR and TSD characteristics and classified into families following the convention used by Han et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Han, Y., Qin, S. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Comparison of class 2 transposable elements at superfamily resolution reveals conserved and distinct features in cereal grass genomes. BMC Genomics 14, 71 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3202">75</a></sup>. The terminal 50 bases of TEs were used as blast queries to identify TE junctions in the pineapple genome. Blast results were filtered to retain hits that have minimum alignment length of 15 bp and are within 10 bp of the TE termini. Multiple blast hits within a window of 30 bp were merged and considered as one junction. These blast hits mark unique TE junctions in the reference pineapple genome. The presence/absence of TE junctions were scored in the 89 accessions based on mapping of Illumina reads from the accessions to the reference pineapple genome. A site was marked as present in an accession when at least one read covered 20 bp upstream and downstream of the TE junction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Identifying tracks of homozygosity</h3><p>Tracks of homozygosity were identified using the 50 resequenced varieties with the highest coverage. Long tracts of homozygosity are usually genomic regions having consecutive genes without heterozygosity. We first identified all the tracts of homozygosity spanning more than three consecutive genes. In the rare cases where two tracts of homozygosity were interrupted by only one gene with only one heterozygous SNP, the three parts were still joined into longer tracts of homozygosity. Then, the number of homozygosity tracts spanning six or more consecutive genes were counted and the summation of homozygosity tract numbers among the 38 cultivars was displayed, with 100 genes as a bin size. Red dotted lines mark predicted centromere locations, based on the observation that by far the highest density of LTR retrotransposons is always found to be flanking the centromere in all studied angiosperm genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Wang, H. The contributions of transposable elements to the structure, function, and evolution of plant genomes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 505–530 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d47403e3215">18</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The <i>bracteatus</i> CB5 genome and annotation, the revised version of F153 genome, and pineapple RNA-seq data have been submitted to EBI-ENA under the study <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB33121">PRJEB33121</a>. Quality filtered Illumina reads for the 89 resequenced pineapple genomes have been deposited in the NCBI BioProject database (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject</a>) under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA389669">PRJNA389669</a>.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>We developed a new algorithm, Pseudohaploid, that identifies and filters out heterozygous contigs based on whole-genome alignment. This method can be run stand-alone with any assembler and is available open-source at <a href="http://github.com/schatzlab/pseudohaploid">http://github.com/schatzlab/pseudohaploid</a>. Other public available open-source and custom software/code used to analyze the data in this study are listed in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0506-8#Sec27">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a>.</p>
